Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD) (Robbins and Monro, 1951) is the de facto standard optimization method in modern machine learning.SGD updates take the following form;wt+1=wt−ηt​gt,subscript𝑤𝑡1subscript𝑤𝑡subscript𝜂𝑡subscript𝑔𝑡w_{t+1}=w_{t}-\eta_{t}g_{t},starting from an arbitrary point w1subscript𝑤1w_{1}, where ηtsubscript𝜂𝑡\eta_{t} is the step-size at time t𝑡t and gtsubscript𝑔𝑡g_{t} is a stochastic gradient at wtsubscript𝑤𝑡w_{t}.The sequence of stepsizes η1,η2,…subscript𝜂1subscript𝜂2…\eta_{1},\eta_{2},\ldots is notoriously hard to tune in machine learning applications (e.g., Bottou, 2012; Schaul et al., 2013), especially when the problem parameters (e.g., smoothness or Lipschitz constant of the objective) or estimates thereof are unknown.This has popularized the study of adaptive gradient methods (e.g., Duchi et al., 2011; Kingma and Ba, 2015; Reddi et al., 2018; Tran et al., 2019; Alacaoglu et al., 2020)where stepsizes are computed adaptively based on magnitudes of previous stochastic gradients.

Although their non-adaptive counterparts are well studied, optimization theory of adaptive methods is still lacking and attract much interest, particularly in the smooth and non-convex setting.The different types of analyses performed by previous works in this setting revolve around the assumptions taken in order to establish convergence which includes the type of noise (bounded variance, sub-Gaussian, bounded support, affine variance, etc.), whether the true gradients or the stochastic gradients are uniformly bounded (Ward et al., 2019; Kavis et al., 2022), and also whether to use a step-size that does not depends on the current stochastic gradient (Li and Orabona, 2019, 2020) or to handle the bias introduced by such a dependency (Ward et al., 2019; Faw et al., 2022).

We focus on perhaps the simplest and most well-studied adaptive optimization method:SGD with AdaGrad-type stepsizes, which we refer to as AdaSGD (also known in the literature as AdaGrad-Norm; e.g.,Ward et al. 2019; Faw et al. 2022).For parameters η,γ>0𝜂𝛾0\eta,\gamma>0, AdaSGD performs SGD updates with stepsizesηt=ηγ2+∑s=1t\norm​gs2subscript𝜂𝑡𝜂superscript𝛾2superscriptsubscript𝑠1𝑡\normsuperscriptsubscript𝑔𝑠2\displaystyle\eta_{t}=\frac{\eta}{\sqrt{\gamma^{2}+\sum_{s=1}^{t}\norm{g_{s}}^{2}}}where gtsubscript𝑔𝑡g_{t} is a stochastic gradient at wtsubscript𝑤𝑡w_{t}.

Although numerous convergence results for smooth AdaSGD exist in the literature (mostly in the non-convex case, except for Li and Orabona (2019) discussed below), they all fall short of at least one important aspect.First, many of the existing analyses require a strong global Lipschitz assumption on the objective (Ward et al., 2019; Zhou et al., 2018; Kavis et al., 2022) which does not hold even for simple quadratic functions in one dimension, and one that is not required for the analysis of analogous non-adaptive methods.Second,several results assume partial knowledge of the problem parameters, such as the smoothness parameter (e.g., Li and Orabona, 2019, 2020), and thus in a sense do not fully establish the desired adaptivity of AdaSGD.A summary of the various results for SGD with AdaGrad stepsizes is presented in Table 1.

To our knowledge, only Faw et al. (2022) do not impose neither of the above limiting assumptions, but their bounds do not hold with high probability: namely, they give convergence bounds with a polynomial dependence on the probability margin (granted, under a somewhat weaker tail assumption on the gradient noise).On the other hand, their results apply in a significantly more challenging setting with an “affine variance” noise model, where the variance of the gradient noise is not uniformly bounded and may scale with the squared norm of the true gradient.

We provide a comprehensive analysis of AdaSGD that does not suffer from any of the former issues:for a β𝛽\beta-smooth objective and under a general affine variance noise model, we establish high probability convergence guarantees in both the non-convex and convex regimes, without any knowledge of the problem parameters and without imposing a global Lipschitz condition on the objective nor a bound on the distance to a minimizer.Further, our analysis is considerably simpler than previous analyses of AdaSGD in similar generality (e.g., Faw et al., 2022).

In more detail, we consider a stochastic first-order model with affine variance noise, similar to the one of Faw et al. (2022); given a point w∈ℝd𝑤superscriptℝ𝑑w\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d} we assume that the gradient oracle returns a random vector g​(w)𝑔𝑤g(w) such that𝔼​[g​(w)∣w]=∇f​(w)𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑔𝑤𝑤∇𝑓𝑤\mathbb{E}[g(w)\mid w]=\nabla f(w)and, with probability one, satisfies111While our analysis assumes for simplicity affine noise bounded with probability one, we provide a general reduction to allow for sub-Gaussian noise with bounded affine variance; here the dependence on 1/γ1𝛾1/\gamma is polynomial).The formal statement is provided in Theorem 2.These convergence bounds match the rates of the form O​(1/T+σ0/T)𝑂1𝑇subscript𝜎0𝑇O(1/T+\sigma_{0}/\sqrt{T}) for non-adaptive, properly tuned SGD in the convex (Lan, 2012) and non-convex cases (Ghadimi and Lan, 2013) cases, up to problem dependent constants and logarithmic factors.However, we emphasize again that our results hold true, with high probability, without assuming uniformly bounded gradients and without requiring any knowledge of the problem parameters (β,σ0,σ1𝛽subscript𝜎0subscript𝜎1\beta,\sigma_{0},\sigma_{1}).To our knowledge, in the non-convex case our result is the first convergence guarantee to hold with high probability (with a proper poly-log dependence on the probability margin) without requiring a global Lipschitz assumption—and in fact, it holds even in a more general affine variance noise model.Similarly, as far as we are aware, our result in the convex case constitutes the first (high probability) analysis of adaptive SGD updates that applies in an unconstrained optimization setup, i.e., without restricting the updates to a domain of bounded (and a-priori known) diameter using projections.Further, our bounds maintain a remarkable property of the non-adaptive bounds for smooth SGD (Lan, 2012; Ghadimi and Lan, 2013):as can be seen from the bounds above,they exhibit optimal rates (in the context of non-accelerated gradient methods) in both the high-noise and low-noise regimes, in the sense that they improve from the traditional stochastic 1/T1𝑇1/\sqrt{T} rate to a fast 1/T1𝑇1/T rate, up to logarithmic factors and problem-dependent constants, when the additive noise parameter σ0subscript𝜎0\sigma_{0} is sufficiently small: namely, when σ0=O​(1/T)subscript𝜎0𝑂1𝑇\sigma_{0}=O(1/\sqrt{T}).In contrast to the non-adaptive bounds, however, this interpolation between different rates occurs automatically, without knowledge of the underlying parameters and their relevant regime.Unlike prior work on the affine noise model, our rate-interpolation takes place even when the affine term is large, i.e., when σ1≫0much-greater-thansubscript𝜎10\sigma_{1}\gg 0; for comparison, Faw et al. (2022) required that σ1=O​(1/T)subscript𝜎1𝑂1𝑇\sigma_{1}=O(1/\sqrt{T}) for drawing a similar conclusion.For completeness, we also provide a high probability analysis of non-adaptive, smooth SGD (with known parameters) under the affine noise model, complementing the result in expectation of Bottou et al. (2018, Theorem 4.8); see details in Appendix C.Finally, it is worth highlighting another remarkable adaptivity property of AdaSGD, demonstrated by our results: the algorithm is able to automatically adapt to the optimization problem at hand and the same step-size schedule simultaneously achieves the optimal rates of convergence of (non-accelerated) SGD in both the convex and non-convex smooth (even unconstrained) optimization settings.1.2 Analysis overviewLet us highlight some of the key challenges in analyzing SGD with adaptive stepsizes and outline our approach for overcoming them.We begin with the non-convex smooth case, which has been the focus of prior work.Non-convex regret analysis.The starting point of our analysis is a “non-convex regret bound” for AdaSGD, similar to Kavis et al. They established that a properly tuned T𝑇T-steps SGD obtain a rate of O​\brk​1∕T+σ∕T𝑂\brk1𝑇𝜎𝑇O\brk{\ifrac{1}{T}+\ifrac{\sigma}{\sqrt{T}}} assuming uniformly bounded variance of σ2superscript𝜎2\sigma^{2}. A tight lower bound is given by Arjevani et al. (2022).Bottou et al. (2018, Theorem 4.8) extended the convergence result for the affine variance noise model.Adaptive methods for non-convex smooth optimization.An extensive line of work tackles convergence guarantees in expectation.222We include here results with polynomial dependence in the probability margin as they can be derived using Markov’s inequality.Ward et al. (2019) gave a rate of O~​\brk​1∕T~𝑂\brk1𝑇\smash{\widetilde{O}}\brk{\ifrac{1}{\sqrt{T}}} for T𝑇T-steps Adaptive SGD, assuming uniformly bounded gradients.In parallel, Li and Orabona (2019) obtained a rate of O~​\brk​1∕T~𝑂\brk1𝑇\smash{\widetilde{O}}\brk{\ifrac{1}{\sqrt{T}}} without assuming uniformly bounded gradients, assuming instead knowledge of the smoothness parameter and using a step-size that does not depend on the current stochastic gradient.Li and Orabona (2019) also obtain a convergence guarantee for the smooth and convex case, again assuming knowledge of the smoothness.A recent work by Faw et al. (2022) assumed affine variance noise and achieved the desired O~​\brk​1∕T~𝑂\brk1𝑇\smash{\widetilde{O}}\brk{\ifrac{1}{\sqrt{T}}} in the noisy regime without assuming uniformly bounded gradients.High probability convergence bounds.Given knowledge of the smoothness and sub-Gaussian noise bounds, Ghadimi and Lan (2013) established convergence with high probability for non-adaptive SGD with properly tuned step-size.Few high probability results of adaptive methods exist.Zhou et al. (2018) and Li and Orabona (2020) established a high probability convergence result for AdaGrad and delayed AdaGrad respectively, assuming sub-Gaussian noise and knowledge of the smoothness to tune the step-size. Kavis et al. (2022) obtained a high probability convergence for Adaptive SGD, assuming uniformly bounded gradients and either sub-Gaussian noise or uniformly bounded stochastic gradients.Assuming heavy-tailed noise, Cutkosky and Mehta (2021) obtained convergence with high probability for normalized SGD with clipping and momentum with a tuned step-size according to a moment bound of the noise.Unconstrained stochastic convex optimization.Also related to our work are Harvey et al. (2019) and Carmon and Hinder (2022) who focused on the (non-smooth) unconstrained and convex setting.Harvey et al. (2019) established convergence with high probability of SGD using a generalized freedman’s inequality.Carmon and Hinder (2022) devised a stochastic parameter-free method which obtain high probability convergence guarantee and their method also obtain optimal rate for noiseless convex and smooth objective.Both papers used a careful martingale analysis in order to bound the distance of the iterates from a minimizer, a desired property for unconstrained stochastic optimization which we also establish for AdaSGD.Parameter-free methods for online optimization.The closely related literature on online optimization includes a plethora of adaptive methods.Recent work in this context is mostly concerned with adaptivity to the norm of the comparator (e.g., Orabona and Pál, 2016; Cutkosky and Orabona, 2018; Mhammedi and Koolen, 2020) and to the scale of the objective (e.g., Orabona and Pál, 2018; Mhammedi and Koolen, 2020).2 PreliminariesWe consider unconstrained smooth optimization in d𝑑d-dimensional Euclidean space ℝdsuperscriptℝ𝑑{\mathbb{R}}^{d} with the ℓ2subscriptℓ2\ell_{2} norm, denoted \norm⋅\norm{\cdot}.A function f𝑓f is said to be β𝛽\beta-smooth if for all x,y∈ℝd𝑥𝑦superscriptℝ𝑑x,y\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d} it holds that \norm​∇f​(x)−∇f​(y)≤β​\norm​x−y\norm∇𝑓𝑥∇𝑓𝑦𝛽\norm𝑥𝑦\norm{\nabla f(x)-\nabla f(y)}\leq\beta\norm{x-y}.This implies, in particular, that for all x,y∈ℝd𝑥𝑦superscriptℝ𝑑x,y\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d}, the following bound is true: f​(y)≤f​(x)+∇f​(x)⋅(y−x)+12​β​\norm​y−x2𝑓𝑦𝑓𝑥bold-⋅∇𝑓𝑥𝑦𝑥12𝛽\norm𝑦superscript𝑥2f(y)\leq f(x)+\nabla f(x)\bm{\cdot}(y-x)+\frac{1}{2}\beta\norm{y-x}^{2}.We additionally assume that f𝑓f is lower bounded over ℝdsuperscriptℝ𝑑{\mathbb{R}}^{d}, and admits a minimum f⋆=minw∈ℝd⁡f​(w)superscript𝑓⋆subscript𝑤superscriptℝ𝑑𝑓𝑤f^{\star}=\min_{w\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d}}f(w).We assume a standard stochastic first-order model, in which we are provided with an oracle g​(w)𝑔𝑤g(w) where, given a point w∈ℝd𝑤superscriptℝ𝑑w\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d} returns a random vector g​(w)∈ℝd𝑔𝑤superscriptℝ𝑑g(w)\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d} such that 𝔼​[g​(w)∣w]=∇f​(w)𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑔𝑤𝑤∇𝑓𝑤\mathbb{E}[g(w)\mid w]=\nabla f(w).Affine noise model.We assume a general noise model, referred to in the literature as the “affine variance noise” (e.g., Faw et al. (2022) and references therein).In this model, the gradient oracle g𝑔g satisfies∀w∈ℝd:\normg(w)−∇f(w)2≤σ02+σ12\norm∇f(w)2\displaystyle\forall~{}w\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d}:\quad\norm{g(w)-\nabla f(w)}^{2}\leq\sigma_{0}^{2}+\sigma_{1}^{2}\norm{\nabla f(w)}^{2}(deterministically), where σ0,σ1≥0subscript𝜎0subscript𝜎10\sigma_{0},\sigma_{1}\geq 0 are noise parameters.For simplicity, we will assume throughout a deterministic bound on the noise magnitude, as stated above; in Appendix A, through a generic reduction, we extend our arguments to apply in a more general sub-Gaussian noise model with affine variance.The affine noise model permits the noise magnitude to grow as the norm of the true gradient increases (without the affine term, i.e., assuming σ1=0subscript𝜎10\sigma_{1}=0, the gradients are effectively “noiseless” for gradients of large magnitude).We refer to Faw et al. (2022) for an additional discussion of this model.Adaptive stepsize Stochastic Gradient Descent (AdaSGD).Starting from an arbitrary point w1∈ℝdsubscript𝑤1superscriptℝ𝑑w_{1}\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d}, for parameters η,γ>0𝜂𝛾0\eta,\gamma>0, the update rule of AdaSGD iswt+1subscript𝑤𝑡1\displaystyle w_{t+1}=wt−ηt​gt,absentsubscript𝑤𝑡subscript𝜂𝑡subscript𝑔𝑡\displaystyle=w_{t}-\eta_{t}g_{t},where gt=g​(wt)subscript𝑔𝑡𝑔subscript𝑤𝑡g_{t}=g(w_{t}) is a stochastic gradient at wtsubscript𝑤𝑡w_{t} and the step-size ηtsubscript𝜂𝑡\eta_{t} is given by∀1≤t≤T:ηt=ηγ2+∑s=1t\norm​gs2.\displaystyle\forall~{}1\leq t\leq T:\qquad\eta_{t}=\frac{\eta}{\sqrt{\gamma^{2}+\sum_{s=1}^{t}\norm{g_{s}}^{2}}}.(1)Additional notation.Throughout, we use 𝔼t−1\brk[s]∗⋅≜𝔼\brk[s]⋅∣g1,…,gt−1\mathbb{E}_{t-1}\brk[s]*{\cdot}\triangleq\mathbb{E}\brk[s]{\cdot\mid g_{1},\ldots,g_{t-1}} to denote the conditional expectation with respect to the stochastic gradients queried up to (not including) step t𝑡t. to the previous section, the bound applies to the case of bounded affine noise, and we defer the extension to sub-Gaussian noise to Theorem 4 in Appendix A.Few additional remarks are in order.First, note that when σ0→0→subscript𝜎00\sigma_{0}\rightarrow 0 we obtain a rate of order 1/T1𝑇1/T, matching the optimal rate for non-stochastic smooth optimization (note that this holds even when σ1≫0much-greater-thansubscript𝜎10\sigma_{1}\gg 0).Second, unlike the result in the non-convex case, the bound here contains a polynomial dependence on 1/γ1𝛾1/\gamma (in the D𝐷D term).This is not an artifact of our analysis: setting a overly small γ𝛾\gamma can in fact lead AdaSGD to gain a distance of Ω​(T)Ω𝑇\Omega(T) to a minimizer; see Appendix B for more details.That said, the algorithm is fairly robust to the choice of γ𝛾\gamma; for example, consider the noisy regime with no affine noise (i.e., σ0≫0much-greater-thansubscript𝜎00\sigma_{0}\gg 0, σ1=0subscript𝜎10\sigma_{1}=0): setting η=Θ​(\norm​w1−w⋆)𝜂Θ\normsubscript𝑤1superscript𝑤⋆\eta=\Theta(\norm{w_{1}-w^{\star}})and any γ𝛾\gamma within the wide (2022).The following lemma is a reduction from X𝑋X to a new bounded random vector with zero-mean which is equal to X𝑋X with high probability.Lemma 9.For any δ∈(0,1)𝛿01\delta\in(0,1) there exist a Let us highlight some of the key challenges in analyzing SGD with adaptive stepsizes and outline our approach for overcoming them.We begin with the non-convex smooth case, which has been the focus of prior work.

The starting point of our analysis is a “non-convex regret bound” for AdaSGD, similar to Kavis et al. (2022);for a β𝛽\beta-smooth objective f𝑓f it can be shown (see Lemma 1 for details and a proof) that∑t=1T∇f​(wt)⋅gtsuperscriptsubscript𝑡1𝑇bold-⋅∇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑡subscript𝑔𝑡\displaystyle\sum_{t=1}^{T}\nabla f(w_{t})\bm{\cdot}g_{t}≤γ​Δ1η+\brk∗2​Δ¯Tη+η​β​∑t=1T\norm​gt2,absent𝛾subscriptΔ1𝜂\brk2subscript¯Δ𝑇𝜂𝜂𝛽superscriptsubscript𝑡1𝑇\normsuperscriptsubscript𝑔𝑡2\displaystyle\leq\frac{\gamma\Delta_{1}}{\eta}+\brk*{\frac{2\bar{\Delta}_{T}}{\eta}+\eta\beta}\sqrt{\sum_{t=1}^{T}\norm{g_{t}}^{2}},where Δ1=f​(w1)−f⋆subscriptΔ1𝑓subscript𝑤1superscript𝑓⋆\Delta_{1}=f(w_{1})-f^{\star} and Δ¯T=maxt≤T⁡f​(wt)−f⋆subscript¯Δ𝑇subscript𝑡𝑇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑡superscript𝑓⋆\bar{\Delta}_{T}=\max_{t\leq T}f(w_{t})-f^{\star}.

The challenging term to handle in this analysis turns out to be the one that involves Δ¯Tsubscript¯Δ𝑇\bar{\Delta}_{T}.Indeed, assume for example a deterministic bound Δ¯T≤Fsubscript¯Δ𝑇𝐹\bar{\Delta}_{T}\leq F (e.g., this is the case whenever f𝑓f is upper bounded uniformly); taking expectations of both sides, we obtain∑t=1Tsuperscriptsubscript𝑡1𝑇\displaystyle\sum_{t=1}^{T}𝔼​\norm​∇f​(wt)2≤γ​Δ1η+\brk∗2​Fη+η​β​∑t=1T𝔼​\norm​gt2.𝔼\norm∇𝑓superscriptsubscript𝑤𝑡2𝛾subscriptΔ1𝜂\brk2𝐹𝜂𝜂𝛽superscriptsubscript𝑡1𝑇𝔼\normsuperscriptsubscript𝑔𝑡2\displaystyle\mathbb{E}\norm{\nabla f(w_{t})}^{2}\leq\frac{\gamma\Delta_{1}}{\eta}+\brk*{\frac{2F}{\eta}+\eta\beta}\sqrt{\sum_{t=1}^{T}\mathbb{E}\norm{g_{t}}^{2}}.Then, using \norm​gt2≤2​\norm​∇f​(wt)−gt2+2​\norm​∇f​(wt)2\normsuperscriptsubscript𝑔𝑡22\norm∇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑡superscriptsubscript𝑔𝑡22\norm∇𝑓superscriptsubscript𝑤𝑡2\norm{g_{t}}^{2}\leq 2\norm{\nabla f(w_{t})-g_{t}}^{2}+2\norm{\nabla f(w_{t})}^{2} and our assumption on the noise yields the bound𝔼​\brk​[s]​3​1T​∑t=1T\norm​∇f​(wt)2𝔼\brkdelimited-[]𝑠31𝑇superscriptsubscript𝑡1𝑇\norm∇𝑓superscriptsubscript𝑤𝑡2\displaystyle\mathbb{E}\brk[s]3{\frac{1}{T}\sum_{t=1}^{T}\norm{\nabla f(w_{t})}^{2}}≤2​γ​Δ1η​T+(1+σ12)​\brk∗2​Fη+η​β2​1T+\brk∗2​Fη+η​β​σ0​8T,absent2𝛾subscriptΔ1𝜂𝑇1superscriptsubscript𝜎12\brk2𝐹𝜂𝜂superscript𝛽21𝑇\brk2𝐹𝜂𝜂𝛽subscript𝜎08𝑇\displaystyle\leq\frac{2\gamma\Delta_{1}}{\eta T}+(1+\sigma_{1}^{2})\brk*{\frac{2F}{\eta}+\eta\beta}^{2}\frac{1}{T}+\brk*{\frac{2F}{\eta}+\eta\beta}\sigma_{0}\sqrt{\frac{8}{T}},which concludes an in-expectation analysis.

The issue, however, is that Δ¯Tsubscript¯Δ𝑇\bar{\Delta}_{T} is a random variable which is not bounded a-priori.Due to the probabilistic dependence between the different factors in the regret bound, our approach proceeds by establishing a bound over Δ¯Tsubscript¯Δ𝑇\bar{\Delta}_{T} that holds with high probability (rather than in expectation).Due to smoothness, we can bound Δ¯Tsubscript¯Δ𝑇\bar{\Delta}_{T} viaf​(wt+1)𝑓subscript𝑤𝑡1\displaystyle f(w_{t+1})≤f​(w1)−∑s=1tηs​∇f​(ws)⋅gs+β2​∑s=1tηs2​\norm​gs2.absent𝑓subscript𝑤1superscriptsubscript𝑠1𝑡bold-⋅subscript𝜂𝑠∇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑠subscript𝑔𝑠𝛽2superscriptsubscript𝑠1𝑡superscriptsubscript𝜂𝑠2\normsuperscriptsubscript𝑔𝑠2\displaystyle\leq f(w_{1})-\sum_{s=1}^{t}\eta_{s}\nabla f(w_{s})\bm{\cdot}g_{s}+\frac{\beta}{2}\sum_{s=1}^{t}\eta_{s}^{2}\norm{g_{s}}^{2}.The second summation in the bound is straightforward to handle and can be bounded (deterministically) by a logarithmic term (see Lemma 6 for details).The first summation is more problematic, again due to (conditional) probabilistic dependencies involving the stepsize ηssubscript𝜂𝑠\eta_{s}, which is crucially a random variable that depends on stochastic gradient gssubscript𝑔𝑠g_{s}.

To break the correlation between ηssubscript𝜂𝑠\eta_{s} and gssubscript𝑔𝑠g_{s} in the second term above, Kavis et al. (2022) replace ηssubscript𝜂𝑠\eta_{s} with ηs−1subscript𝜂𝑠1\eta_{s-1}.Then, for bounding the difference between ηssubscript𝜂𝑠\eta_{s} and ηs−1subscript𝜂𝑠1\eta_{s-1} they assumed a global Lipschitz assumption which limits the effect of each individual gradient on the stepsize ηssubscript𝜂𝑠\eta_{s}.To avoid this limiting assumption, we use a different “decorrelated” proxy for the stepsize, defined by replacing \norm​gt2\normsuperscriptsubscript𝑔𝑡2\norm{g_{t}}^{2} with (1+σ12)​\norm​∇f​(wt)2+σ021superscriptsubscript𝜎12\norm∇𝑓superscriptsubscript𝑤𝑡2superscriptsubscript𝜎02(1+\sigma_{1}^{2})\norm{\nabla f(w_{t})}^{2}+\sigma_{0}^{2};this was introduced first by Ward et al. (2019) and then modified by Faw et al. (2022) to allow for affine-variance noise.We can then decompose using the proxy,−ηs​∇f​(ws)⋅gsbold-⋅subscript𝜂𝑠∇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑠subscript𝑔𝑠\displaystyle-\eta_{s}\nabla f(w_{s})\bm{\cdot}g_{s}=−η~s​\norm​∇f​(ws)2+η~s​∇f​(ws)⋅(∇f​(ws)−gs)+(η~s−ηs)​∇f​(ws)⋅gs,absentsubscript~𝜂𝑠\norm∇𝑓superscriptsubscript𝑤𝑠2bold-⋅subscript~𝜂𝑠∇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑠∇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑠subscript𝑔𝑠bold-⋅subscript~𝜂𝑠subscript𝜂𝑠∇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑠subscript𝑔𝑠\displaystyle=-\tilde{\eta}_{s}\norm{\nabla f(w_{s})}^{2}+\tilde{\eta}_{s}\nabla f(w_{s})\bm{\cdot}(\nabla f(w_{s})-g_{s})+(\tilde{\eta}_{s}-\eta_{s})\nabla f(w_{s})\bm{\cdot}g_{s},and bound the cumulative sum of each of the terms.

The challenging term to bound is the martingale∑s=1tη~s​∇f​(ws)⋅(∇f​(ws)−gs).superscriptsubscript𝑠1𝑡bold-⋅subscript~𝜂𝑠∇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑠∇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑠subscript𝑔𝑠\displaystyle\sum_{s=1}^{t}\tilde{\eta}_{s}\nabla f(w_{s})\bm{\cdot}(\nabla f(w_{s})-g_{s}).Bounding the martingale is challenging because the magnitude \norm​∇f​(ws)−gs\norm∇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑠subscript𝑔𝑠\norm{\nabla f(w_{s})-g_{s}} depends on the previous steps with affine-variance noise.The key observation is that since we wish to establish a bound for f​(wt+1)−f⋆𝑓subscript𝑤𝑡1superscript𝑓⋆f(w_{t+1})-f^{\star} for all t𝑡t, and thanks to the well-known property of smooth functions that \norm​∇f​(w)2≤2​β​(f​(w)−f⋆)\norm∇𝑓superscript𝑤22𝛽𝑓𝑤superscript𝑓⋆\norm{\nabla f(w)}^{2}\leq 2\beta(f(w)-f^{\star}), it is suffice to perform an inductive argument and use the bound for the previous steps to bound the affine noise terms.As the noise bound in a given step is in itself a random variable that depends on previous stochastic gradients, we appeal to a Bernstein-type martingale concentration inequality (specifically, we use one due to Li and Orabona 2020) and a careful analysis yields a bound on −∑s=1tηs​∇f​(ws)⋅gssuperscriptsubscript𝑠1𝑡bold-⋅subscript𝜂𝑠∇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑠subscript𝑔𝑠-\sum_{s=1}^{t}\eta_{s}\nabla f(w_{s})\bm{\cdot}g_{s} and establishes a bound on Δ¯Tsubscript¯Δ𝑇\bar{\Delta}_{T} without the need for a global Lipschitz assumption.

We follow a similar approach using a regret bound and a bound of \norm​wt−w⋆2\normsubscript𝑤𝑡superscriptsuperscript𝑤⋆2\norm{w_{t}-w^{\star}}^{2} which holds for all t≤T𝑡𝑇t\leq T with high probability.In the convex case we need to perform a similar decomposition to bound −∑s=1tηs​gs⋅(ws−w⋆)superscriptsubscript𝑠1𝑡bold-⋅subscript𝜂𝑠subscript𝑔𝑠subscript𝑤𝑠superscript𝑤⋆-\sum_{s=1}^{t}\eta_{s}g_{s}\bm{\cdot}(w_{s}-w^{\star}) where w⋆superscript𝑤⋆w^{\star} is a minimizer of f𝑓f.Using the proxy stepsize sequence η~tsubscript~𝜂𝑡\tilde{\eta}_{t}does not work.Instead, we employ a slightly different decorrelated stepsize sequence (η^s)ssubscriptsubscript^𝜂𝑠𝑠(\hat{\eta}_{s})_{s}that (A) suffices for bounding ∑s=1t(η^s−ηs)​gs⋅(ws−w⋆)superscriptsubscript𝑠1𝑡bold-⋅subscript^𝜂𝑠subscript𝜂𝑠subscript𝑔𝑠subscript𝑤𝑠superscript𝑤⋆\sum_{s=1}^{t}(\hat{\eta}_{s}-\eta_{s})g_{s}\bm{\cdot}(w_{s}-w^{\star}) and (B) keeps the norm of the zero-mean vector η^s​(∇f​(ws)−gs)subscript^𝜂𝑠∇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑠subscript𝑔𝑠\hat{\eta}_{s}(\nabla f(w_{s})-g_{s}) bounded with probability one, which is crucial for the martingale analysis.The martingale lemma of Li and Orabona (2020) is too loose for bounding ∑s=1tη^s​(∇f​(ws)−gs)⋅(ws−w⋆)superscriptsubscript𝑠1𝑡bold-⋅subscript^𝜂𝑠∇𝑓subscript𝑤𝑠subscript𝑔𝑠subscript𝑤𝑠superscript𝑤⋆\sum_{s=1}^{t}\hat{\eta}_{s}(\nabla f(w_{s})-g_{s})\bm{\cdot}(w_{s}-w^{\star}) and we rely instead on the martingale analysis technique of Carmon and Hinder (2022) which uses an empirical Bernstein inequality introduced by Howard et al. (2021).The empirical Bernstein is necessary in order to tightly bound the martingaleand establish the bound on \norm​wt−w⋆2\normsubscript𝑤𝑡superscriptsuperscript𝑤⋆2\norm{w_{t}-w^{\star}}^{2}.

The first to analyze SGD in the smooth non-convex setting and obtain tight convergence bounds were Ghadimi and Lan (2013). They established that a properly tuned T𝑇T-steps SGD obtain a rate of O​\brk​1∕T+σ∕T𝑂\brk1𝑇𝜎𝑇O\brk{\ifrac{1}{T}+\ifrac{\sigma}{\sqrt{T}}} assuming uniformly bounded variance of σ2superscript𝜎2\sigma^{2}. A tight lower bound is given by Arjevani et al. (2022).Bottou et al. (2018, Theorem 4.8) extended the convergence result for the affine variance noise model.

An extensive line of work tackles convergence guarantees in expectation.222We include here results with polynomial dependence in the probability margin as they can be derived using Markov’s inequality.Ward et al. (2019) gave a rate of O~​\brk​1∕T~𝑂\brk1𝑇\smash{\widetilde{O}}\brk{\ifrac{1}{\sqrt{T}}} for T𝑇T-steps Adaptive SGD, assuming uniformly bounded gradients.In parallel, Li and Orabona (2019) obtained a rate of O~​\brk​1∕T~𝑂\brk1𝑇\smash{\widetilde{O}}\brk{\ifrac{1}{\sqrt{T}}} without assuming uniformly bounded gradients, assuming instead knowledge of the smoothness parameter and using a step-size that does not depend on the current stochastic gradient.Li and Orabona (2019) also obtain a convergence guarantee for the smooth and convex case, again assuming knowledge of the smoothness.A recent work by Faw et al. (2022) assumed affine variance noise and achieved the desired O~​\brk​1∕T~𝑂\brk1𝑇\smash{\widetilde{O}}\brk{\ifrac{1}{\sqrt{T}}} in the noisy regime without assuming uniformly bounded gradients.

Given knowledge of the smoothness and sub-Gaussian noise bounds, Ghadimi and Lan (2013) established convergence with high probability for non-adaptive SGD with properly tuned step-size.Few high probability results of adaptive methods exist.Zhou et al. (2018) and Li and Orabona (2020) established a high probability convergence result for AdaGrad and delayed AdaGrad respectively, assuming sub-Gaussian noise and knowledge of the smoothness to tune the step-size. Kavis et al. (2022) obtained a high probability convergence for Adaptive SGD, assuming uniformly bounded gradients and either sub-Gaussian noise or uniformly bounded stochastic gradients.Assuming heavy-tailed noise, Cutkosky and Mehta (2021) obtained convergence with high probability for normalized SGD with clipping and momentum with a tuned step-size according to a moment bound of the noise.

Also related to our work are Harvey et al. (2019) and Carmon and Hinder (2022) who focused on the (non-smooth) unconstrained and convex setting.Harvey et al. (2019) established convergence with high probability of SGD using a generalized freedman’s inequality.Carmon and Hinder (2022) devised a stochastic parameter-free method which obtain high probability convergence guarantee and their method also obtain optimal rate for noiseless convex and smooth objective.Both papers used a careful martingale analysis in order to bound the distance of the iterates from a minimizer, a desired property for unconstrained stochastic optimization which we also establish for AdaSGD.

The closely related literature on online optimization includes a plethora of adaptive methods.Recent work in this context is mostly concerned with adaptivity to the norm of the comparator (e.g., Orabona and Pál, 2016; Cutkosky and Orabona, 2018; Mhammedi and Koolen, 2020) and to the scale of the objective (e.g., Orabona and Pál, 2018; Mhammedi and Koolen, 2020).

We consider unconstrained smooth optimization in d𝑑d-dimensional Euclidean space ℝdsuperscriptℝ𝑑{\mathbb{R}}^{d} with the ℓ2subscriptℓ2\ell_{2} norm, denoted \norm⋅\norm{\cdot}.A function f𝑓f is said to be β𝛽\beta-smooth if for all x,y∈ℝd𝑥𝑦superscriptℝ𝑑x,y\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d} it holds that \norm​∇f​(x)−∇f​(y)≤β​\norm​x−y\norm∇𝑓𝑥∇𝑓𝑦𝛽\norm𝑥𝑦\norm{\nabla f(x)-\nabla f(y)}\leq\beta\norm{x-y}.This implies, in particular, that for all x,y∈ℝd𝑥𝑦superscriptℝ𝑑x,y\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d}, the following bound is true: f​(y)≤f​(x)+∇f​(x)⋅(y−x)+12​β​\norm​y−x2𝑓𝑦𝑓𝑥bold-⋅∇𝑓𝑥𝑦𝑥12𝛽\norm𝑦superscript𝑥2f(y)\leq f(x)+\nabla f(x)\bm{\cdot}(y-x)+\frac{1}{2}\beta\norm{y-x}^{2}.We additionally assume that f𝑓f is lower bounded over ℝdsuperscriptℝ𝑑{\mathbb{R}}^{d}, and admits a minimum f⋆=minw∈ℝd⁡f​(w)superscript𝑓⋆subscript𝑤superscriptℝ𝑑𝑓𝑤f^{\star}=\min_{w\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d}}f(w).We assume a standard stochastic first-order model, in which we are provided with an oracle g​(w)𝑔𝑤g(w) where, given a point w∈ℝd𝑤superscriptℝ𝑑w\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d} returns a random vector g​(w)∈ℝd𝑔𝑤superscriptℝ𝑑g(w)\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d} such that 𝔼​[g​(w)∣w]=∇f​(w)𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑔𝑤𝑤∇𝑓𝑤\mathbb{E}[g(w)\mid w]=\nabla f(w).

We assume a general noise model, referred to in the literature as the “affine variance noise” (e.g., Faw et al. (2022) and references therein).In this model, the gradient oracle g𝑔g satisfies∀w∈ℝd:\normg(w)−∇f(w)2≤σ02+σ12\norm∇f(w)2\displaystyle\forall~{}w\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d}:\quad\norm{g(w)-\nabla f(w)}^{2}\leq\sigma_{0}^{2}+\sigma_{1}^{2}\norm{\nabla f(w)}^{2}(deterministically), where σ0,σ1≥0subscript𝜎0subscript𝜎10\sigma_{0},\sigma_{1}\geq 0 are noise parameters.For simplicity, we will assume throughout a deterministic bound on the noise magnitude, as stated above; in Appendix A, through a generic reduction, we extend our arguments to apply in a more general sub-Gaussian noise model with affine variance.

The affine noise model permits the noise magnitude to grow as the norm of the true gradient increases (without the affine term, i.e., assuming σ1=0subscript𝜎10\sigma_{1}=0, the gradients are effectively “noiseless” for gradients of large magnitude).We refer to Faw et al. (2022) for an additional discussion of this model.

Starting from an arbitrary point w1∈ℝdsubscript𝑤1superscriptℝ𝑑w_{1}\in{\mathbb{R}}^{d}, for parameters η,γ>0𝜂𝛾0\eta,\gamma>0, the update rule of AdaSGD iswt+1subscript𝑤𝑡1\displaystyle w_{t+1}=wt−ηt​gt,absentsubscript𝑤𝑡subscript𝜂𝑡subscript𝑔𝑡\displaystyle=w_{t}-\eta_{t}g_{t},where gt=g​(wt)subscript𝑔𝑡𝑔subscript𝑤𝑡g_{t}=g(w_{t}) is a stochastic gradient at wtsubscript𝑤𝑡w_{t} and the step-size ηtsubscript𝜂𝑡\eta_{t} is given by∀1≤t≤T:ηt=ηγ2+∑s=1t\norm​gs2.\displaystyle\forall~{}1\leq t\leq T:\qquad\eta_{t}=\frac{\eta}{\sqrt{\gamma^{2}+\sum_{s=1}^{t}\norm{g_{s}}^{2}}}.(1)

Throughout, we use 𝔼t−1\brk[s]∗⋅≜𝔼\brk[s]⋅∣g1,…,gt−1\mathbb{E}_{t-1}\brk[s]*{\cdot}\triangleq\mathbb{E}\brk[s]{\cdot\mid g_{1},\ldots,g_{t-1}} to denote the conditional expectation with respect to the stochastic gradients queried up to (not including) step t𝑡t. We use log\log to denote the base 222 logarithm.

First, we focus on the general smooth case without convexity assumptions.Our main result of this section is the following convergence bound for AdaSGD.

As stated, the bound in the theorem holds for bounded affine noise; for the precise bound in the more general sub-Gaussian case, see Theorem 3 in Appendix A.

In addition to the features of this convergence result already highlighted in the introduction, we also note the high probability guarantee it provides that the maximal optimality gap f​(wt)−f⋆𝑓subscript𝑤𝑡superscript𝑓⋆f(w_{t})-f^{\star} remains O~​(1)~𝑂1\smash{\widetilde{O}}(1) along the trajectory of AdaSGD.Comparing the result to the high probability analysis when the parameters are known (Appendix C), the main difference is the bound of f​(wt)−f⋆𝑓subscript𝑤𝑡superscript𝑓⋆f(w_{t})-f^{\star} which is O​(Δ1)𝑂subscriptΔ1O(\Delta_{1}) when the parameters are known (for large enough T𝑇T), while the bound of Theorem 1 has additional terms that depend on σ0subscript𝜎0\sigma_{0} and σ1subscript𝜎1\sigma_{1}.We remark that the prior results of Faw et al. (2022) in this setting include a somewhat worse high-order noise term, of order σ1​(σ02+σ112)∕Tsubscript𝜎1superscriptsubscript𝜎02superscriptsubscript𝜎112𝑇\ifrac{\sigma_{1}(\sigma_{0}^{2}+\sigma_{1}^{12})}{\sqrt{T}} in the noisy regime (for σ1=ω​(1)subscript𝜎1𝜔1\sigma_{1}=\omega(1)).

Note that the Δ¯Tsubscript¯Δ𝑇\bar{\Delta}_{T} term in the right hand-side of the bound is a random variable.In the noiseless setting with known smoothness and properly tuned stepsize, we know that Δ¯T=Δ1subscript¯Δ𝑇subscriptΔ1\bar{\Delta}_{T}=\Delta_{1} since Δt+1≤ΔtsubscriptΔ𝑡1subscriptΔ𝑡\Delta_{t+1}\leq\Delta_{t} for all t𝑡t and proving convergence would be much simplified.The next lemma bounds Δ¯T=maxs≤t⁡f​(ws)−f⋆subscript¯Δ𝑇subscript𝑠𝑡𝑓subscript𝑤𝑠superscript𝑓⋆\bar{\Delta}_{T}=\max_{s\leq t}f(w_{s})-f^{\star} with high probability in the noisy case.

For our high probability analysis we use the following martingale concentration inequality (Li and Orabona, 2020, Lemma 1).

In this section we give a convergence analysis for AdaSGD provided that the objective f𝑓f is a convex and β𝛽\beta-smooth function over ℝdsuperscriptℝ𝑑{\mathbb{R}}^{d}.We prove the following:

Similarly to the previous section, the bound applies to the case of bounded affine noise, and we defer the extension to sub-Gaussian noise to Theorem 4 in Appendix A.

Few additional remarks are in order.First, note that when σ0→0→subscript𝜎00\sigma_{0}\rightarrow 0 we obtain a rate of order 1/T1𝑇1/T, matching the optimal rate for non-stochastic smooth optimization (note that this holds even when σ1≫0much-greater-thansubscript𝜎10\sigma_{1}\gg 0).Second, unlike the result in the non-convex case, the bound here contains a polynomial dependence on 1/γ1𝛾1/\gamma (in the D𝐷D term).This is not an artifact of our analysis: setting a overly small γ𝛾\gamma can in fact lead AdaSGD to gain a distance of Ω​(T)Ω𝑇\Omega(T) to a minimizer; see Appendix B for more details.That said, the algorithm is fairly robust to the choice of γ𝛾\gamma; for example, consider the noisy regime with no affine noise (i.e., σ0≫0much-greater-thansubscript𝜎00\sigma_{0}\gg 0, σ1=0subscript𝜎10\sigma_{1}=0): setting η=Θ​(\norm​w1−w⋆)𝜂Θ\normsubscript𝑤1superscript𝑤⋆\eta=\Theta(\norm{w_{1}-w^{\star}})and any γ𝛾\gamma within the wide rangeγ∈\brk​[s]​σ0,σ0​T𝛾\brkdelimited-[]𝑠subscript𝜎0subscript𝜎0𝑇\gamma\in\brk[s]{\sigma_{0},\sigma_{0}\sqrt{T}}leads to the optimal rate (up to logarithmic factors).
